# UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt
# Date: November 2, 2004
# Author: Ken Whistler
#
# Updated February 23, 2012 to fix a minor typo.
#
# Note: This is a completely artificially reconstituted
# UnicodeData.txt file, projecting back to what UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt
# might have appeared like, if the conventions of the Unicode 2.0
# data file had been in place and had been used for Unicode 1.0.
#
# This data file is of historical interest only, for people interested
# in the history of encoding of Unicode characters. It should *not*
# be used to attempt a Unicode 1.0 implementation, as such data was
# never actually available or used for such Unicode 1.0 implementations
# as existed prior to the release of Unicode 1.1.
#
# Character code points and names accurately reflect Unicode 1.0.
# All other fields are projections back from the UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt
# values, if available, or UnicodeData-2.0.14.txt, if not yet specified
# in UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt. (The latter would apply for projections
# for Tibetan characters, for example, which were in Unicode 1.0,
# but removed from Unicode 1.1, and recoded in Unicode 2.0, with a
# very different repertoire and organization.)
#
# The major distinctions between UnicodeData-1.0.0.txt and
# UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt are as follows:
#
#   1. UnicodeData-1.0.0.txt follows Unicode 1.0, Volume 1
#      (published in October, 1991)
#      and omits the Han character additions that were published
#      in Volume 2.
#
#   2. UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt adds the Han characters from Volume 2
#      (published in June, 1992).
#
#   3. UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt reflects the block range adjustment
#      for the Private Use Area.
#
#   4. UnicodeData-1.0.1.txt notes the deletion of 6 Unicode 1.0.0
#      characters which were not included in the merged repertoire
#      with 10646-1, but does not yet reflect the character name
#      changes or character additions, which were not published
#      until Unicode 1.1.
#
#   5. The single exception for the name changes is for U+FEFF
#      ZERO WIDTH NO-BREAK SPACE (former BYTE ORDER MARK), which
#      is called out and discussed explicitly in Volume 2.
#
# Considerations regarding decomposition mappings:
#   Decomposition mappings use the *syntax* of Unicode 2.0.
#   Decomposition mappings also follow the recursive mapping
#     theory of Unicode 2.0 and ff, rather than the full
#     decompositions shown in UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt.
#   Decomposition mappings retain the <compat> tag from
#     Unicode 2.0, even though the distinction between
#     canonical and compatibility decompositions was not yet
#     formulated for UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt. Other tags
#     not introduced until Unicode 2.0 are also retained, as
#     much as possible.
#   However, in cases such as overstruck bars and slashes, which
#     were decomposed in UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt, but not in
#     Unicode 2.0 and later, the decomposition mappings follows
#     UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt, not anticipating the Unicode 2.0
#     revision in theory.
#   In a few cases, outright, obvious errors from UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt,
#     such as the decompositions for U+22E0 and U+22E1, are simply
#     retained for historical interest.
#
# Considerations regarding default case mappings:
#   Where possible, the default case mappings follow
#     UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt, even where mappings were later
#     corrected, so as not to anticipate later changes.
#
# Considerations regarding general category and bidirectional
# category values:
#   These values follow UnicodeData-1.1.5.txt, even where
#     values were later corrected, so as not to anticipate
#     the later changes. The general category values were
#     heavily reviewed and revised during the development
#     of Unicode 2.0, but there is no sense in pretending
#     that that analysis preceded Unicode 1.1.
#
# Considerations regarding the ISO comment field:
#   The ISO comment field technically should be empty for all
#     entries, since its addition to UnicodeData.txt was
#     designed to track notes that needed to be printed in
#     ISO 10646 itself. However, it is used here simply to
#     keep such annotations and/or aliases from Unicode 1.0
#     which may help in identifying characters. No other
#     status should be ascribed to the field.
#
# Considerations regarding Hangul syllables:
#   Unicode 1.0 had 2350 Hangul syllable character assigned.
#     However, names for those characters were never published.
#     If they had had names published, they would have followed
#     the pattern seen in the circled Hangul syllable characters.
#     Thus, the name for U+3400 would have been:
#         3400 HANGUL SYLLABLE GA
#     However, rather than go through the exercise of reconstructing
#     unpublished names for all 2350 syllables, and in the interest
#     of maintaining easier comparability, the 2350 entries are listed
#     in this data file using the Unicode 1.1 Hangul syllable names.
#     Thus, the name for U+3400 as shown in this data file is:    
#         3400 HANGUL SYLLABLE KIYEOK A 
#     Reconstructing what would have been Unicode 1.0 Hangul
#     syllable character names, had they ever existed, is left
#     as an exercise for the reader.
#
